We can compute the circumcircle by using the condition that the points satisfy a circle equation: x^2+y^2 + Ax + By + C = 0. Since A=0 is not necessarily on the circle, but we can plug B, E, F.

Let’s do that: Let circle equation: x^2+y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0. For point B=(2,0): 4 + 2D + F = 0 => 2D + F = -4. (1)
For E=(λ p_x, λ p_y): λ^2(p_x^2+p_y^2) + D λ p_x + E λ p_y + F = 0 => λ^2 P·P + λ (D p_x + E p_y) + F = 0. (2)
For F=(μ p_x, μ p_y): μ^2 P·P + μ (D p_x + E p_y) + F = 0. (3)

We have three equations in D, E, F. Subtract (2) and (3) to eliminate F: (μ^2 - λ^2)P·P + (μ - λ)(D p_x + E p_y) = 0 => (μ - λ)[(μ+λ)P·P + (D p_x+E p_y)] = 0. Since μ ≠ λ (E and F distinct), we have (μ+λ)P·P + (D p_x+E p_y) = 0 => D p_x+E p_y = -(μ+λ)P·P. (4)

Now from (2): λ^2 P·P + λ (D p_x+E p_y) + F = 0 => λ^2 P·P + λ (-(μ+λ)P·P) + F = 0 => λ^2 P·P - λμ P·P - λ^2 P·P + F = 0 => -λμ P·P + F = 0 => F = λμ P·P. (5)

Now from (1): 2D + F = -4 => 2D + λμ P·P = -4 => D = (-4 - λμ P·P)/2. (6)

Now we also have (4): D p_x+E p_y = -(μ+λ)P·P. So we can solve for E once D known: E p_y = -(μ+λ)P·P - D p_x. So E = [-(μ+λ)P·P - D p_x]/p_y, provided p_y ≠ 0. So we have the circle equation.

Now the center O = (-D/2, -E/2). So O = ( -D/2, -E/2 ). And radius squared = O^2 - F? Actually for circle x^2+y^2+Dx+Ey+F=0, center = (-D/2, -E/2), radius^2 = (D^2+E^2)/4 - F. So we can compute.

Now line L: through H = (x_H, y_P) parallel to P. So L: (x_H + t p_x, y_P + t p_y). We need to show that L is tangent to circle (BEF). That is, the distance from O to L equals radius.

The distance from point O to line L: line L has direction vector P = (p_x, p_y). So a normal vector to L is perpendicular to P. For line L: (x - x_H, y - y_P) cross P = 0? Actually line L: param eq. The distance from O to L = |(O - H) × P| / |P|, where × denotes 2D cross product (determinant). So we need to show that |(O - H) × P| / |P| = radius.

Alternatively, we can check that the line L is tangent if and only if the equation for intersection (substituting L into circle equation) has a double root. That is, the quadratic in t from plugging (x_H + t p_x, y_P + t p_y) into circle equation has discriminant zero. That might be simpler: we have circle equation: x^2+y^2+Dx+Ey+F=0. Plug x = x_H + t p_x, y = y_P + t p_y. Then we get a quadratic in t: (p_x^2+p_y^2) t^2 + 2( x_H p_x + y_P p_y ) t + (x_H^2+y_P^2 + D x_H + E y_P + F) = 0. For tangency, discriminant = 0: [2( x_H p_x + y_P p_y )]^2 - 4 (p_x^2+p_y^2) (x_H^2+y_P^2 + D x_H + E y_P + F) = 0 => (x_H p_x + y_P p_y)^2 - (p_x^2+p_y^2)(x_H^2+y_P^2 + D x_H + E y_P + F) = 0.

So we need to verify that this holds given our expressions.

Given the complexity, maybe there is a synthetic insight that reduces the work. Let's think synthetically.

We have circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. M and N centers. C on Ω, D on Γ on line MN with order C-M-N-D. So C and D are the points where the line of centers meets the circles on the opposite sides of M and N respectively. So MC = r, ND = R, and MN = d.

P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D lie on line MN, the perpendicular bisector of CD is perpendicular to MN, i.e., parallel to AB. So P lies on a line through the midpoint of CD parallel to AB. Also note that A is on both circles, and P is circumcenter of ACD. So P is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD. Since M is center of Ω, and C is on Ω, the line MC is radius. The perpendicular bisector of AC: not directly.

But maybe we can relate P to the radical axis or something. Consider the circle with diameter CD? Not.

Another approach: Use the fact that P is the center of the circle through A, C, D. So AP = CP = DP. Since C and D are on line MN, we have CP = DP, so P lies on the perpendicular bisector of CD, which is line through midpoint of CD perpendicular to MN. So P is on that line. Also, note that M and N are on MN, so maybe the reflections of M and N across P? Or maybe H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN, so H is the reflection of P across MN? Actually in a triangle, the orthocenter and vertices have relationships. For triangle PMN, with M and N on a line (MN), the altitude from P is perpendicular to MN, so H lies on that altitude. So H is the intersection of the altitude from P (which is perpendicular to MN) and the altitude from M (which is perpendicular to PN). So H is not simply the reflection of P across MN.

But perhaps we can find that the line through H parallel to AP is actually the line through H that is the reflection of AP across the line through H perpendicular to something? Not.

Maybe we can use coordinates but with a clever parameterization that simplifies. Since we have A at origin and AB on x-axis, we have c=1, m and n parameters. Then we can compute P, H, λ, μ, etc. Let's attempt to do the algebra systematically.

Set c=1. Then:

r^2 = 1 + m^2, R^2 = 1 + n^2. So r = sqrt(1+m^2), R = sqrt(1+n^2). Note that m and n are real numbers with m < n and |m| < |n|? Actually we have r < R, so m^2 < n^2, so |m| < |n|. Since m < n, we have m < n, and if both positive, then m < n, and |m|<|n| holds; if both negative, then m < n but both negative, say m = -3, n = -2, then |m|=3, |n|=2, so |m|>|n|, but r<R would require m^2 < n^2 => 9<4 false, so cannot have both negative. If m negative and n positive, then m^2 < n^2 possible if |m|<n. So m could be negative, n positive, or both positive. So m < n, and m^2 < n^2. So m could be negative or positive. We'll keep general.

Now define:

y_C = m - r, y_D = n + R. So C = (1, y_C), D = (1, y_D).

Now P = (x_P, y_P) where:

x_P = 1/2 - (y_C y_D)/(2*1) = 1/2 - (y_C y_D)/2. So x_P = (1 - y_C y_D)/2.

y_P = (y_C + y_D)/2 = (m - r + n + R)/2 = (m + n + R - r)/2.

Now we also have r = sqrt(1+m^2), R = sqrt(1+n^2). So these are functions of m,n.

Now compute λ = t_E = 2 (P·M)/(P·P). M = (1,m). So P·M = x_P*1 + y_P*m = x_P + m y_P.

Similarly μ = 2 (P·N)/(P·P) with N=(1,n): P·N = x_P + n y_P.

And P·P = x_P^2 + y_P^2.

Now E = λ P, F = μ P.

Now B = (2,0). So we have circle through B, E, F.

Now we need H = orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N have same x-coordinate 1, the line MN is vertical. So altitude from P is horizontal: y = y_P. So H = (x_H, y_P). We need x_H. Compute using altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. Vector PN = N - P = (1 - x_P, n - y_P). So direction of altitude from M is perpendicular to PN: d = (n - y_P, -(1 - x_P)) (since dot with PN = (1-x_P)(n-y_P)+(n-y_P)(-(1-x_P))=0). So altitude from M: (1,m) + s*(n-y_P, -(1-x_P)). Intersection with y=y_P gives: m + s*(-(1-x_P)) = y_P => m - s(1-x_P) = y_P => s = (m - y_P)/(1-x_P). Then x_H = 1 + s*(n-y_P) = 1 + (m - y_P)(n-y_P)/(1-x_P). So H = ( 1 + (m - y_P)(n-y_P)/(1-x_P) , y_P ).

Now line L through H parallel to AP: AP direction is P (since A=0). So L: H + t P.

We need to show that L is tangent to circle (BEF). That is, the distance from O (center of circle (BEF)) to L equals radius. We can compute O from earlier formulas.

Let’s compute P·P, P·M, P·N, λ, μ in terms of m,n. Then compute D, E, F for circle (BEF). Then compute O = (-D/2, -E/2). Then compute the discriminant condition. This is algebraic but we can try to simplify.

First compute y_C and y_D:

y_C = m - r = m - sqrt(1+m^2)
y_D = n + R = n + sqrt(1+n^2)

We have r = sqrt(1+m^2), R = sqrt(1+n^2).

Now note that y_C is negative? Since sqrt(1+m^2) > |m|, so m - sqrt(1+m^2) ≤ 0? For m positive, m - sqrt(1+m^2) < 0; for m negative, say m=-3, sqrt(10)=3.16, then -3-3.16=-6.16 <0. So y_C < 0 always. Similarly y_D > 0 always. So y_C negative, y_D positive.

Now x_P = (1 - y_C y_D)/2. Since y_C y_D is negative (product of negative and positive), so - y_C y_D is positive, so x_P > 1/2? Actually 1 - (negative) = 1 + positive > 1, so x_P > 1/2? Let's compute: y_C y_D negative, so -y_C y_D positive, so 1 - y_C y_D = 1 + positive > 1, so x_P > 1/2. Could be >1? Possibly.

Thus O_x = 1 + (P·M)(P·N)/S.

Now O_y = -E/2 = - [-(μ+λ)S - D p_x]/(2 p_y) = ( (μ+λ)S + D p_x )/(2 p_y).

Now (μ+λ)S = (2 (P·M)/S + 2 (P·N)/S)*S = 2(P·M) + 2(P·N) = 2(P·M+P·N).

Let’s denote:

- Let O1 be the midpoint of CD. Since C and D lie on line MN, O1 is on MN. And O1 is the foot of the perpendicular from P to MN? Actually P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD, which is line through O1 perpendicular to MN. So PO1 ⟂ MN. So PO1 is parallel to AB.

- Since M and N are on MN, and P is on line through O1 perpendicular to MN, we have that triangle PMN: the altitude from P is PO1 (since PO1 ⟂ MN). So H lies on PO1. Actually H is orthocenter, so it lies on the altitude from P, which is line through P perpendicular to MN. So H is on line PO1. So H, P, O1 are collinear, and PO1 ⟂ MN. So H is on line through P perpendicular to MN. So H is on the line through P parallel to AB (since AB ⟂ MN). So H lies on line through P parallel to AB. Also note that O1 is the midpoint of CD.

Now AP is a line through A and P. So line through H parallel to AP is a line through H parallel to AP. Since H is on line through P parallel to AB, and AP is some line, maybe we can find that this line is actually the reflection of AP across the line through H perpendicular to something.

Perhaps we can show that the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter something, and that the line through H parallel to AP is the Simson line of some point on that circle. For a point X on circle (BEF), the Simson line with respect to triangle BEF is the line through the feet of perpendiculars from X to sides. If we can show that H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN, and that AP is the line through A and P, maybe we can find a point X on circle (BEF) such that its Simson line is the line through H parallel to AP. That would imply that the line is tangent? Actually the Simson line of a point on the circumcircle is not generally tangent; it's a line through the feet. But if we consider the point A? A is on both circles, but is A on circle (BEF)? B, E, F: A is on line EF, but not necessarily on circle (BEF). However maybe A is the orthocenter of triangle BEF? Not.

Let's compute some specific case to see the geometry. Choose convenient numbers: Let M=(0,0)? Actually we set A=0, B=2? Let's pick a concrete example to get intuition.

Let’s set A=0, B=2. Then c=1. Choose m=1, then r = sqrt(1+1)=√2≈1.414. So M=(1,1). Choose n=2, then R = sqrt(1+4)=√5≈2.236. So N=(1,2). Then C = (1, m - r) = (1, 1-1.414)= (1, -0.414). D = (1, n+R) = (1, 2+2.236)= (1, 4.236). Then triangle ACD: A(0,0), C(1,-0.414), D(1,4.236). Compute P: x_P = 1/2 - (y_C y_D)/2 = 0.5 - ((-0.414)*4.236)/2 = 0.5 - (-1.753)/2 = 0.5 + 0.8765 = 1.3765. y_P = (y_C+y_D)/2 = (-0.414+4.236)/2 = 3.822/2 = 1.911. So P≈(1.3765,1.911). Then line AP: through origin and P, so direction (1.3765,1.911). Compute E and F: λ = 2(P·M)/S, μ = 2(P·N)/S. Compute P·M = 1.3765*1 + 1.911*1 = 1.3765+1.911=3.2875. S = 1.3765^2+1.911^2 = 1.894+3.652=5.546. So λ = 2*3.2875/5.546 = 6.575/5.546≈1.184. So E = λP = (1.3765*1.184, 1.911*1.184) = (1.629, 2.262). μ = 2(P·N)/S = 2*(1.3765*1+1.911*2)/5.546 = 2*(1.3765+3.822)/5.546 = 2*5.1985/5.546 = 10.397/5.546≈1.873. So F = μP = (1.3765*1.873, 1.911*1.873) = (2.577, 3.579). B=(2,0). So triangle BEF: B(2,0), E(1.629,2.262), F(2.577,3.579). Compute circumcenter O of BEF. We can compute using perpendicular bisectors. Midpoint of BE: ((2+1.629)/2=1.8145, (0+2.262)/2=1.131). Slope of BE: (2.262-0)/(1.629-2) = 2.262/(-0.371) = -6.098. Perpendicular slope = 1/6.098≈0.164. Equation: y-1.131 = 0.164(x-1.8145). Midpoint of BF: ((2+2.577)/2=2.2885, (0+3.579)/2=1.7895). Slope of BF: (3.579-0)/(2.577-2)=3.579/0.577=6.204. Perpendicular slope = -1/6.204≈-0.161. Equation: y-1.7895 = -0.161(x-2.2885). Solve for intersection. From first: y = 1.131 + 0.164(x-1.8145) = 1.131 + 0.164x - 0.297 = 0.834 + 0.164x. From second: y = 1.7895 -0.161(x-2.2885) = 1.7895 -0.161x + 0.368 = 2.1575 -0.161x. Set equal: 0.834+0.164x = 2.1575-0.161x => 0.164x+0.161x = 2.1575-0.834 => 0.325x = 1.3235 => x = 4.074. Then y = 0.834+0.164*4.074 = 0.834+0.668=1.502. So O≈(4.074,1.502). Radius squared: distance from O to B: (4.074-2)^2+(1.502-0)^2 = (2.074)^2+1.502^2 = 4.301+2.256=6.557. So radius≈2.56.

Now compute H: orthocenter of triangle PMN. M=(1,1), N=(1,2), P=(1.3765,1.911). Since MN vertical, altitude from P is horizontal: y=y_P=1.911. Altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. PN = N-P = (1-1.3765, 2-1.911)=(-0.3765,0.089). So direction of PN = (-0.3765,0.089). A perpendicular direction = (0.089,0.3765) (since dot = -0.3765*0.089+0.089*0.3765=0). So altitude from M: through M(1,1) with direction (0.089,0.3765). Param: (1,1)+s(0.089,0.3765). Intersection with y=1.911 gives: 1+0.3765s = 1.911 => s = 0.5/0.3765? Actually 1.911-1=0.911, so s = 0.911/0.3765 ≈ 2.419. Then x_H = 1 + 0.089*2.419 = 1 + 0.215 = 1.215. So H≈(1.215,1.911). Now line through H parallel to AP: AP direction = P = (1.3765,1.911). So L: (1.215,1.911)+t(1.3765,1.911). We need to check if L is tangent to circle (BEF). Compute distance from O to L. Compute vector O-H = (4.074-1.215, 1.502-1.911) = (2.859, -0.409). Cross product with direction P: (2.859, -0.409) × (1.3765,1.911) = 2.859*1.911 - (-0.409)*1.3765 = 5.460 + 0.563 = 6.023. |P| = sqrt(1.3765^2+1.911^2)=sqrt(1.894+3.652)=sqrt(5.546)=2.355. So distance = 6.023/2.355 = 2.56. That equals radius! So indeed L is tangent. So the property holds.

Now we have a concrete example. The algebra should work out.

Given the complexity, we can attempt to prove the tangency using analytic geometry but with a clever parameterization that reduces the algebra. Since we have A=0, B=2 (or B=1), we can use complex numbers with A=0, B=1? Let's set A=0, B=1 for simplicity. Then the circles through 0 and 1 have centers on line Re(z)=1/2. Let M = 1/2 + i m, N = 1/2 + i n. Then r = |M| = sqrt(1/4+m^2), R = |N| = sqrt(1/4+n^2). Then C = 1/2 + i (m - r), D = 1/2 + i (n+R). Then P is circumcenter of triangle ACD (0, C, D). Since A=0, the circumcenter is given by (C D (\overline{C} - \overline{D}))/(C \overline{D} - \overline{C} D). But maybe we can compute P in terms of m,n. Let's do that.

Let c = 1/2 + i y_C, d = 1/2 + i y_D, where y_C = m - r, y_D = n+R. Then P = (c d (\overline{c} - \overline{d}))/(c \overline{d} - \overline{c} d). Compute using coordinates: c = (1/2, y_C), d = (1/2, y_D). Then c d = (1/2 + i y_C)(1/2 + i y_D) = 1/4 + i (y_C+y_D)/2 - y_C y_D. And \overline{c} - \overline{d} = (1/2 - i y_C) - (1/2 - i y_D) = -i(y_C - y_D). So numerator = c d * (-i(y_C - y_D)) = -i (y_C - y_D) c d. Denominator: c \overline{d} - \overline{c} d = (1/2 + i y_C)(1/2 - i y_D) - (1/2 - i y_C)(1/2 + i y_D) = Expand: (1/2)(1/2) + (1/2)(-i y_D) + i y_C (1/2) + i y_C (-i y_D) - [ (1/2)(1/2) + (1/2)(i y_D) - i y_C (1/2) - i y_C (i y_D) ] = (1/4 - i y_D/2 + i y_C/2 + y_C y_D) - (1/4 + i y_D/2 - i y_C/2 + y_C y_D) = (1/4 - i y_D/2 + i y_C/2 + y_C y_D - 1/4 - i y_D/2 + i y_C/2 - y_C y_D) = (-i y_D/2 + i y_C/2 - i y_D/2 + i y_C/2) = i(y_C - y_D). So denominator = i(y_C - y_D). Thus P = (-i (y_C - y_D) c d) / (i(y_C - y_D)) = - c d. So P = - c d. That is a nice result! Let's verify: P = -c d. Indeed, for A=0, C=c, D=d, the circumcenter is given by (c d (\overline{c} - \overline{d}))/(c \overline{d} - \overline{c} d). Imag = (ap - bq)(q-b) + (aq+bp)(a-p)

Actually the perpendicular bisector is perpendicular to AC and passes through the midpoint. So any line perpendicular to AC is parallel to the perpendicular bisector. So the line through M perpendicular to AC is parallel to the perpendicular bisector, but not necessarily the same line unless M lies on the perpendicular bisector. Since M is on the perpendicular bisector, the line through M perpendicular to AC is exactly the perpendicular bisector (because it's perpendicular to AC and passes through M which is on the perpendicular bisector, but the perpendicular bisector is a line; if M is on it, then the line through M perpendicular to AC is the same line as the perpendicular bisector because both are perpendicular to AC and contain M). Indeed, the perpendicular bisector of AC is the unique line perpendicular to AC that passes through the midpoint of AC. Since M lies on that line, the line through M perpendicular to AC is exactly that line (because there is only one line through M perpendicular to AC). So the perpendicular bisector of AC is the line through M perpendicular to AC. Similarly, the perpendicular bisector of AD is the line through N perpendicular to AD.

Now P is the intersection of these two perpendicular bisectors. So P is the intersection of the line through M perpendicular to AC and the line through N perpendicular to AD. So P is determined by these conditions.

Now AP is the line through A and P. Since P lies on perpendicular bisector of AC, we have PA = PC. Similarly PA = PD.

Now consider the orthocenter H of triangle PMN. Since M and N are on line MN, the altitude from P is perpendicular to MN. So H lies on the line through P perpendicular to MN. That line is parallel to AB (since AB ⟂ MN). So H lies on line through P parallel to AB.

Now we want to show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). So we need to relate circle (BEF) to something.

Maybe we can show that B, E, F are concyclic with some other points, and that the line through H parallel to AP is the radical axis of two circles that are tangent? Or perhaps we can show that the circle (BEF) is the image of the circle (ACD) under some homothety or inversion.

Let's explore the geometry of points E and F. Since A, E, F are collinear on line AP, and E is on Ω, F on Γ, we have that A is a common point of circles Ω and Γ, and also of line EF. So the line AP is the common chord of the circles? Actually the common chord of Ω and Γ is AB, not AP. So AP is a line through A that meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. So E and F are points on the circles such that A, E, F are collinear. So the line AP is a line through A that cuts the circles at A and another point. So we have that the power of point P with respect to Ω? Not.

Now consider the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Since B is on both circles, and E on Ω, F on Γ, we might find that the circle (BEF) is the circle that is the image of the circle (ACD) under some transformation. For instance, consider the homothety with center A that sends Ω to Γ? But that would send E to F? Actually if we take the homothety centered at A that sends Ω to Γ, then it sends B to itself? Not. But there is a spiral similarity that sends Ω to Γ and sends A to A? Actually the spiral similarity that sends Ω to Γ and fixes A would send the other intersection B to some point on Γ? Not.

Alternatively, consider the inversion at A that sends circles to lines. Under inversion, Ω becomes line ℓ_Ω, Γ becomes line ℓ_Γ, and the line AP becomes itself (since it passes through A). The points E and F map to points E' and F' on ℓ_Ω and ℓ_Γ respectively, and A maps to infinity? Actually inversion: A is center, so A maps to infinity? No, the center of inversion is not mapped; it's removed. So we consider the transformation on the plane minus A. Under inversion, the circle Ω (through A) becomes a line not passing through A, call ℓ_Ω. Similarly Γ becomes ℓ_Γ. The line AP (through A) becomes itself (since it passes through A, it maps to itself, but A is removed). The points C and D are on Ω and Γ respectively, so their images C' and D' lie on ℓ_Ω and ℓ_Γ. Also, the circumcircle of ACD (which passes through A) becomes a line, call ℓ_{ACD}, which is the image of the circle (ACD). Since P is the circumcenter of ACD, under inversion, P maps to some point P'? Not directly.

Maybe we can choose the inversion radius such that the circle (ACD) becomes a line that is the perpendicular bisector of something? Not.

Given the algebraic approach seems manageable with the formulas we derived, we can attempt to prove the tangency using analytic geometry with the coordinate system we set: A=0, B=2c, M=(c,m), N=(c,n), with c>0, and r^2=c^2+m^2, R^2=c^2+n^2, and m<n, and |m|<|n|? Actually from r<R, we have m^2 < n^2. So |m|<|n|. Since m<n, we have either m negative, n positive, or both positive with m<n. But we can keep general.

We have:

y_C = m - r
y_D = n + R
x_P = (c^2 - y_C y_D)/(2c)
y_P = (y_C + y_D)/2

We also have:

t_E = 2(x_P c + y_P m)/S, where S = x_P^2 + y_P^2
t_F = 2(x_P c + y_P n)/S

Then E = t_E P, F = t_F P.

We also have H = (c + (m-y_P)(n-y_P)/(c-x_P), y_P).

We need to show that the line L: H + t P is tangent to circle through B=(2c,0), E, F.

Now we can compute the circumcircle of BEF. Since E and F are on line through origin with direction P, we can use the earlier derived formulas for the circle equation: x^2+y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0, with D = -2 - 2 (P·M)(P·N)/S? Wait we derived D = -2 - 2 (P·M)(P·N)/S? That was with c=1? Let's re-derive for general c.

We have B=(2c,0). E = t_E P, F = t_F P. Let’s denote P = (p_x, p_y). Then we have:

For point B: (2c)^2 + 0^2 + D(2c) + E*0 + F = 0 => 4c^2 + 2cD + F = 0 => 2cD + F = -4c^2. (1)

For point E: t_E^2 S + D t_E p_x + E t_E p_y + F = 0. (2)
For point F: t_F^2 S + D t_F p_x + E t_F p_y + F = 0. (3)

Subtract (2) and (3): (t_F^2 - t_E^2)S + (t_F - t_E)(D p_x + E p_y) = 0 => (t_F - t_E)[(t_F+t_E)S + (D p_x+E p_y)] = 0. Since t_F ≠ t_E, we have (t_F+t_E)S + (D p_x+E p_y) = 0 => D p_x + E p_y = -(t_F+t_E)S. (4)

Now from (2): t_E^2 S + t_E (D p_x+E p_y) + F = 0 => t_E^2 S + t_E (-(t_F+t_E)S) + F = 0 => t_E^2 S - t_E t_F S - t_E^2 S + F = 0 => - t_E t_F S + F = 0 => F = t_E t_F S. (5)

Now from (1): 2cD + t_E t_F S = -4c^2 => D = (-4c^2 - t_E t_F S)/(2c). (6)

Now we also have (4): D p_x + E p_y = -(t_F+t_E)S. So we can solve for E: E p_y = -(t_F+t_E)S - D p_x => E = [ -(t_F+t_E)S - D p_x ] / p_y, provided p_y ≠ 0.

Now the center O = (-D/2, -E/2). So O_x = -D/2 = (4c^2 + t_E t_F S)/(4c). O_y = -E/2 = [ (t_F+t_E)S + D p_x ]/(2 p_y). (7)

Now we need to show that the distance from O to line L (through H parallel to P) equals radius R_c = sqrt( (D^2+E^2)/4 - F ). Alternatively, we can use the condition that the line L is tangent if and only if the equation for intersection (substituting L into circle equation) has a double root. That is, the quadratic in t from plugging (x_H + t p_x, y_P + t p_y) into circle equation has discriminant zero. That condition is:

( x_H p_x + y_P p_y )^2 - S ( x_H^2 + y_P^2 + D x_H + E y_P + F ) = 0. (8)

Since we have expressions for D, E, F in terms of t_E, t_F, S, p_x, p_y, and also x_H, y_P in terms of m,n,c, we can attempt to verify (8). This is heavy but maybe we can find simplifications by expressing everything in terms of m,n,c and using the relations r^2=c^2+m^2, R^2=c^2+n^2.

We can compute y_C, y_D in terms of m,n,c. Then compute p_x, p_y, S, t_E, t_F. Then compute x_H. Then verify (8). This is a purely algebraic problem. Since we are to produce a rigorous proof, we can do this algebra by hand, but it requires careful manipulation. However we can try to simplify by using the fact that t_E = 2 (p_x c + p_y m)/S, t_F = 2 (p_x c + p_y n)/S. So t_E t_F = 4 (p_x c + p_y m)(p_x c + p_y n)/S^2. And t_E + t_F = 2 (p_x c + p_y m + p_x c + p_y n)/S = 2 (2p_x c + p_y (m+n))/S = (4p_x c + 2p_y (m+n))/S.

Also note that p_x and p_y are given by:

p_x = (c^2 - y_C y_D)/(2c)
p_y = (y_C + y_D)/2

And y_C = m - r, y_D = n + R, with r = sqrt(c^2+m^2), R = sqrt(c^2+n^2). So these are messy.

Now we need to verify (8). This is a heavy algebraic identity. Perhaps we can simplify by using the fact that t_E = 2 (p_x c + p_y m)/S, t_F = 2 (p_x c + p_y n)/S. However we can try to find a synthetic proof.

Let's try to find a synthetic proof. We have circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. Let M and N be centers. Let C and D be the points where line MN meets Ω and Γ respectively, with order C-M-N-D. So C is on Ω on the side opposite N, D on Γ on the side opposite M. So MC = r, ND = R.

Let P be circumcenter of triangle ACD. So PA = PC = PD.

Now consider the circle with diameter CD. Its center is midpoint of CD, call O_CD. Since C and D are on line MN, O_CD is on MN. Also note that A is a point such that ∠CAD is some angle. The circumcenter P of ACD is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD. Since M is on perpendicular bisector of AC, and N is on perpendicular bisector of AD, we have that M, P, and the midpoint of AC are collinear? Actually the perpendicular bisector of AC is the line through M perpendicular to AC. So P lies on that line. Similarly, P lies on line through N perpendicular to AD. So P is the intersection of these two lines.

Now consider the orthocenter H of triangle PMN. Since M and N are on line MN, the altitude from P is perpendicular to MN. So H lies on that altitude. Also, the altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So H is the intersection of the line through P perpendicular to MN and the line through M perpendicular to PN. So H is the orthocenter.

Now we want to show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). So we need to relate circle (BEF) to something.

Maybe we can consider the circle with diameter AB? Since A and B are intersections, the circle with diameter AB is the circle with center at midpoint of AB, which is on MN? Actually the midpoint of AB is on MN, but not necessarily the center of the circle with diameter AB. The circle with diameter AB has center at midpoint of AB, which is on MN, and radius = AB/2. But we have B, E, F on some circle. Could it be that the circle (BEF) is the circle that is the image of the circle (ACD) under a homothety with center A? Since A is common point, if we take the homothety that sends Ω to Γ, then it sends E to F? Actually if we take the homothety centered at A that sends Ω to Γ, then it sends B to some point on Γ? Not. But there is a spiral similarity that sends Ω to Γ and sends A to A, and it sends B to itself? That would be identity. So not.

Alternatively, consider the inversion at A that sends circles to lines. Under inversion, Ω becomes line ℓ_Ω, Γ becomes line ℓ_Γ, and the line AP becomes itself (since it passes through A). The points C and D map to C' and D' on ℓ_Ω and ℓ_Γ respectively. The circumcircle of ACD becomes line through C' and D'? Actually the circle (ACD) passes through A, so its image is a line not passing through A. Since it passes through A, C, D, its image is the line through C' and D'. Maybe we can choose the inversion radius such that the circle (ACD) becomes a line that is the perpendicular bisector of something? Not.

Given the difficulty, perhaps we can use complex numbers with A=0 and B=1. Then we have M = 1/2 + i m, N = 1/2 + i n. Then we can compute everything in terms of m,n. Then we can verify the tangency condition using complex numbers. This might be simpler because we can use the fact that the condition for a line through H parallel to AP to be tangent to circle (BEF) is equivalent to some cross ratio or power condition. Since we have explicit formulas, we can attempt to compute the condition.

Let's set A=0, B=1. Then the perpendicular bisector of AB is x=1/2. So M = 1/2 + i m, N = 1/2 + i n. Then r = |M| = sqrt(1/4 + m^2), R = |N| = sqrt(1/4 + n^2). Then C = M - r * (0,1)? Actually C is on line MN (vertical line x=1/2) and on Ω, with order C-M-N-D. So C = (1/2, m - r), D = (1/2, n + R). So coordinates: C = (1/2, m - r), D = (1/2, n + R). Then P is circumcenter of triangle ACD (0,0), C, D. Using the formula derived: P = ( (x^2 - y_C y_D)/(2x) , (y_C+y_D)/2 ) with x=1/2, y_C=m-r, y_D=n+R. So p_x = ( (1/4) - (m-r)(n+R) )/(2*(1/2)) = (1/4 - (m-r)(n+R))/1 = 1/4 - (m-r)(n+R). p_y = ( (m-r)+(n+R) )/2 = (m+n + R - r)/2. So P = ( 1/4 - (m-r)(n+R) , (m+n+R-r)/2 ). That is a nice expression.

Now note that r = sqrt(1/4+m^2), R = sqrt(1/4+n^2). So (m-r)(n+R) = (m-r)(n+R). This is messy.

Now we also have E and F: Since A=0, line AP is the line through origin and P, so points are tP. For circle Ω: center M, radius r. Since A=0 is on Ω, we have |M|=r. For X = tP, condition |X-M|^2 = r^2 => t^2|P|^2 - 2t Re(P \overline{M}) + |M|^2 = r^2 => t^2|P|^2 - 2t Re(P \overline{M}) + r^2 = r^2 => t(t|P|^2 - 2 Re(P \overline{M})) = 0 => t=0 or t = 2 Re(P \overline{M})/|P|^2. So t_E = 2 Re(P \overline{M})/|P|^2. Similarly t_F = 2 Re(P \overline{N})/|P|^2. So E = t_E P, F = t_F P.

Now B = 1 (since A=0, B=1). So triangle BEF: B=1, E=t_E P, F=t_F P.

Now H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N have same x-coordinate 1/2, the altitude from P is horizontal line y = p_y. So H = (x_H, p_y). We need x_H. Using altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. Vector PN = N-P = (1/2 - p_x, n - p_y). So direction of altitude from M is perpendicular to PN: d = (n - p_y, -(1/2 - p_x)). So altitude from M: (1/2, m) + s*(n-p_y, -(1/2-p_x)). Intersection with y=p_y gives: m + s*(-(1/2-p_x)) = p_y => m - s(1/2-p_x) = p_y => s = (m - p_y)/(1/2-p_x). Then x_H = 1/2 + s*(n-p_y) = 1/2 + (m-p_y)(n-p_y)/(1/2-p_x). So H = ( 1/2 + (m-p_y)(n-p_y)/(1/2-p_x) , p_y ).

Now line L through H parallel to AP: AP direction is P, so L: H + t P.

We need to show that L is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF (B=1, E, F). Since we have coordinates, we can attempt to compute the condition. But maybe we can find a relation that simplifies using complex numbers.

Let’s use complex numbers: Let A=0, B=1. Then M = 1/2 + i m, N = 1/2 + i n. Let’s denote P = p (complex). Then we have p = 1/4 - (m-r)(n+R) + i (m+n+R-r)/2. But we can also compute p using the formula p = - (c d) with c = 1/2 + i y_C, d = 1/2 + i y_D? Actually earlier we had P = -c d with c = x+iy_C, d = x+iy_D, x=1/2. So p = - (1/2 + i y_C)(1/2 + i y_D) = - (1/4 - y_C y_D + i (y_C+y_D)/2) = (y_C y_D - 1/4) - i (y_C+y_D)/2. But our derived p_x = 1/4 - (m-r)(n+R) = 1/4 - y_C y_D? Since y_C = m-r, y_D = n+R. Then P = (p_x, p_y) = (1/4 - y_C y_D, (y_C+y_D)/2). So p_x = 1/4 - (m-r)(n+R), p_y = (m-r + n+R)/2 = (m+n+R-r)/2.

Now compute S = p_x^2 + p_y^2.

Now compute t_E = 2 Re(P \overline{M})/S. Since M = 1/2 + i m, \overline{M}=1/2 - i m. So Re(P \overline{M}) = p_x*(1/2) + p_y*m. So t_E = 2(p_x/2 + p_y m)/S = (p_x + 2p_y m)/S. Similarly t_F = (p_x + 2p_y n)/S.

Then E = t_E P, F = t_F P.

So (m-p_y)(n-p_y) = ((m-n -R+r)/2)*((n-m -R+r)/2) = ((r-R + (m-n))*(r-R - (m-n)))/4 = ((r-R)^2 - (m-n)^2)/4. So (m-p_y)(n-p_y) = ((r-R)^2 - (m-n)^2)/4.

Also 1/2 - p_x = 1/2 - (1/4 - y_C y_D) = 1/2 - 1/4 + y_C y_D = 1/4 + y_C y_D = 1/4 + (m-r)(n+R). So 1/2 - p_x = 1/4 + (m-r)(n+R).

The midpoint of AB is (u/2, v/2) and lies on MN. So we can take (x0,y0) = (u/2, v/2). So MN: (x,y) = (u/2, v/2) + t * (-v, u). So points on MN: (u/2 - v t, v/2 + u t). So C and D are the two points where this line meets the circle centered at P with radius p. So we have:

| (u/2 - v t, v/2 + u t) - (p,0) |^2 = p^2.

Compute: (u/2 - v t - p)^2 + (v/2 + u t)^2 = p^2.

Expand: (u/2 - p)^2 - 2(u/2 - p) v t + v^2 t^2 + (v/2)^2 + 2(v/2) u t + u^2 t^2 = p^2.

Combine t^2: (u^2+v^2) t^2. Combine t: -2(u/2 - p) v t + 2(v/2) u t = -2v(u/2 - p) t + u v t = -u v t + 2v p t + u v t = 2v p t. So linear term: 2v p t. Constant: (u/2 - p)^2 + (v/2)^2 = (u^2/4 - u p + p^2) + v^2/4 = (u^2+v^2)/4 - u p + p^2.

So equation: (u^2+v^2) t^2 + 2v p t + (u^2+v^2)/4 - u p + p^2 = p^2.

Cancel p^2: (u^2+v^2) t^2 + 2v p t + (u^2+v^2)/4 - u p = 0.

Multiply by 4: 4(u^2+v^2) t^2 + 8v p t + (u^2+v^2) - 4u p = 0.

So the two solutions t_C and t_D correspond to C and D. Their sum and product can be found.

Now we have expressions for M_x, N_x, t_M, t_N, t_C, t_D, p, u, v. This is still messy, but perhaps we can find relations that simplify.

Note that M and N are on MN, so M = (u/2 - v t_M, v/2 + u t_M), N = (u/2 - v t_N, v/2 + u t_N). Then M_x = u/2 - v t_M, N_x = u/2 - v t_N. So E = (2M_x,0) = (u - 2v t_M, 0). Similarly F = (u - 2v t_N, 0). So E and F are points on x-axis.

Now the horizontal line through H is y = λ v. For tangency, we need the distance from O to that line equals radius. That is, |O_y - λ v| = R_c, where R_c is the radius of circle (BEF). Alternatively, we can compute the power of H with respect to the circle: Pow(H) = |H-O|^2 - R_c^2. The condition for the horizontal line through H to be tangent is that the distance from O to that line equals radius. That is equivalent to: (O_y - λ v)^2 = R_c^2. But also note that the horizontal line is y = λ v, so the foot of perpendicular from O is (O_x, λ v). That point lies on the circle if the line is tangent. So we need to show that (O_x, λ v) is on the circle. That is, the distance from O to (O_x, λ v) is |O_y - λ v| = R_c. So we need to show that the point (O_x, λ v) lies on the circle. So we need to verify that (O_x, λ v) satisfies the circle equation. Since we have coordinates, we can compute the equation of the circle through B, E, F. Let's find the circle equation.

Let the circle equation be: x^2 + y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0. Then for point E: (u-2v t_M)^2 + 0 + D(u-2v t_M) + F = 0. For point F: (u-2v t_N)^2 + D(u-2v t_N) + F = 0. For point B: u^2+v^2 + D u + E v + F = 0.

Subtract the two equations for E and F: (u-2v t_M)^2 - (u-2v t_N)^2 + D[(u-2v t_M)-(u-2v t_N)] = 0 => ( (u-2v t_M) - (u-2v t_N) )((u-2v t_M)+(u-2v t_N) + D) = 0 => ( -2v(t_M-t_N) ) ( 2u -2v(t_M+t_N) + D ) = 0 => Since t_M≠t_N (M and N distinct), we have D = -2u + 2v(t_M+t_N). So D = 2v(t_M+t_N) - 2u.

Now from the equation for E: (u-2v t_M)^2 + D(u-2v t_M) + F = 0 => F = - (u-2v t_M)^2 - D(u-2v t_M). So F = - (u-2v t_M)^2 - (2v(t_M+t_N)-2u)(u-2v t_M). We can compute later.

Now from the equation for B: u^2+v^2 + D u + E v + F = 0 => E v = -u^2-v^2 - D u - F. So E = [ -u^2-v^2 - D u - F ] / v.

Now the center O = (-D/2, -E/2). So O_x = -D/2 = - (2v(t_M+t_N)-2u)/2 = -v(t_M+t_N) + u. So O_x = u - v(t_M+t_N). That matches our earlier.

Now the radius squared: R_c^2 = (D^2+E^2)/4 - F.

Now the point of tangency on the horizontal line y = λ v is (O_x, λ v). For this point to lie on the circle, we need:

O_x^2 + (λ v)^2 + D O_x + E (λ v) + F = 0. That is: O_x^2 + λ^2 v^2 + D O_x + E λ v + F = 0.

But we also have that the circle equation is satisfied by (O_x, λ v) if and only if the distance from O to that point equals radius, i.e., |O_y - λ v| = R_c. Since O_y = -E/2, that condition is | -E/2 - λ v| = R_c. Squaring: (E/2 + λ v)^2 = R_c^2 => (E + 2λ v)^2/4 = R_c^2 => (E+2λ v)^2 = 4R_c^2 = D^2+E^2 - 4F. So (E+2λ v)^2 = D^2+E^2 - 4F => Expand: E^2+4λ v E+4λ^2 v^2 = D^2+E^2 - 4F => 4λ v E+4λ^2 v^2 = D^2 - 4F => 4λ v E + 4λ^2 v^2 = D^2 - 4F. So this is a condition that determines λ.

Now we also have that H = (p+λ u, λ v) lies on the altitude from M, which is perpendicular to PN. So we can find λ from that condition. Perhaps we can show that λ satisfies the above equation.

Let's compute PN = N - P = (u/2 - v t_N - p, v/2 + u t_N - 0) = (u/2 - v t_N - p, v/2 + u t_N). The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, so the direction of altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. Since H lies on that altitude, the vector from M to H is parallel to a direction perpendicular to PN. So (H - M) · PN = 0.

Now H = (p+λ u, λ v). M = (u/2 - v t_M, v/2 + u t_M). So H - M = (p+λ u - u/2 + v t_M, λ v - v/2 - u t_M) = (p - u/2 + v t_M + λ u, -v/2 + λ v - u t_M). Compute dot with PN = (u/2 - v t_N - p, v/2 + u t_N). So:

(H-M)·PN = (p - u/2 + v t_M + λ u)*(u/2 - v t_N - p) + (-v/2 + λ v - u t_M)*(v/2 + u t_N) = 0.

We can simplify this equation. This will give a relation that determines λ. Then we need to show that this λ also satisfies the tangency condition. This is still heavy.

Given the time, I think the analytic geometry approach is the most reliable. We can set up coordinates with A=(0,0), B=(2,0) (by scaling and rotation, we can set AB=2). Then M=(1,m), N=(1,n). Given the time, I think it's acceptable to produce an analytic proof. We'll set up coordinates with A=(0,0), B=(2,0) (so AB=2). Then M=(1,m), N=(1,n) with m<n. Then r^2=1+m^2, R^2=1+n^2. We'll compute P, H, E, F, and then verify the tangency condition. To simplify, we can use the fact that the tangency condition is equivalent to the equation of the circle (BEF) and the line L having a double root. We can compute the equation of the circle (BEF) using the formula for the circle through three points. Since E and F are on x-axis, we can find the circle's equation as: (x - x0)^2 + (y - y0)^2 = R^2, where x0 = u - v(t_M+t_N) as before, but we have coordinates in terms of m,n. Let's compute everything explicitly.

Set A=(0,0), B=(2,0). Then M=(1,m), N=(1,n). Then r^2=1+m^2, R^2=1+n^2. Then C and D are on line MN (x=1) with coordinates: C=(1, m - r), D=(1, n+R). Then P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. Using the formula P = ( (x^2 - y_C y_D)/(2x) , (y_C+y_D)/2 ) with x=1, y_C=m-r, y_D=n+R. So p_x = (1 - (m-r)(n+R))/2, p_y = ((m-r)+(n+R))/2 = (m+n+R-r)/2. So P = ( (1 - (m-r)(n+R))/2 , (m+n+R-r)/2 ).

Now compute S = p_x^2 + p_y^2.

Now compute E and F: Since AP is line through A and P, but we need the second intersections with circles. However, we can also compute E and F as the points on line AP such that they lie on circles. But we can also compute them using the fact that the line AP is the line through origin and P. So points are tP. For circle Ω: center M=(1,m), radius r. Condition: |tP - M|^2 = r^2. Since |M|=r, we have t^2|P|^2 - 2t Re(P \overline{M}) + r^2 = r^2 => t(t|P|^2 - 2 Re(P \overline{M}))=0 => t_E = 2 Re(P \overline{M})/|P|^2. So E = t_E P. Similarly t_F = 2 Re(P \overline{N})/|P|^2, F = t_F P.

Now compute H: orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N have same x-coordinate 1, the altitude from P is horizontal line y = p_y. So H = (x_H, p_y). We need x_H. Using altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. Vector PN = N - P = (1 - p_x, n - p_y). So direction of altitude from M is perpendicular to PN: d = (n - p_y, -(1 - p_x)). So altitude from M: (1,m) + s*(n-p_y, -(1-p_x)). Intersection with y=p_y gives: m + s*(-(1-p_x)) = p_y => m - s(1-p_x) = p_y => s = (m - p_y)/(1-p_x). Then x_H = 1 + s*(n-p_y) = 1 + (m-p_y)(n-p_y)/(1-p_x). So H = ( 1 + (m-p_y)(n-p_y)/(1-p_x) , p_y ).

Now we need to prove that the line through H parallel to AP (direction P) is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF. B=(2,0). E and F are as above.

Now we can attempt to verify the condition (8). This is a heavy algebraic identity. However, we can use the fact that the problem is symmetric in some sense, and maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter the segment connecting the reflections of H across something. Expand: (u^2/4 - u v t_M + v^2 t_M^2) + (v^2/4 + u v t_M + u^2 t_M^2) = (u^2+v^2)/4 + (u^2+v^2) t_M^2 = r^2. So (u^2+v^2)(t_M^2 + 1/4) = r^2. So t_M^2 = (r^2/(u^2+v^2)) - 1/4. Similarly, t_N^2 = (R^2/(u^2+v^2)) - 1/4.

Now we also have that P is circumcenter of ACD, so PA=PC=PD. That gave us that C and D are on circle centered at P radius p. So that is satisfied.

Given the difficulty, I think the analytic proof is the way to go. We'll set up coordinates with A=(0,0), B=(2,0). Then we compute everything in terms of m,n. Let's compute everything explicitly.

Now compute t_E = 2 Re(P \overline{M})/S, with M=(1,m). So Re(P \overline{M}) = p_x*1 + p_y*m = p_x + m p_y. So t_E = 2(p_x + m p_y)/S. Similarly t_F = 2(p_x + n p_y)/S.

Then E = t_E P, F = t_F P.

Now H = ( 1 + (m-p_y)(n-p_y)/(1-p_x) , p_y ).

We need to show that line L: H + t P is tangent to circle through B=(2,0), E, F.

Now we can attempt to compute the condition (8). Let's denote P = (p_x, p_y). Then S = p_x^2+p_y^2. Then D = (-4c^2 - t_E t_F S)/(2c) with c=1? Actually B=2, so 2c=2 => c=1. So D = (-4 - t_E t_F S)/2? Wait careful: In the circle equation x^2+y^2+Dx+Ey+F=0, for point B=(2,0): 4+2D+F=0 => 2D+F=-4. For point E: t_E^2 S + D t_E p_x + E t_E p_y + F = 0. For point F: t_F^2 S + D t_F p_x + E t_F p_y + F = 0. Subtracting: (t_F^2 - t_E^2)S + (t_F - t_E)(D p_x + E p_y)=0 => (t_F - t_E)[(t_F+t_E)S + D p_x + E p_y]=0 => D p_x + E p_y = -(t_F+t_E)S. Then from E equation: t_E^2 S + t_E (D p_x+E p_y) + F = 0 => t_E^2 S - t_E (t_F+t_E)S + F = 0 => -t_E t_F S + F = 0 => F = t_E t_F S. Then from B equation: 2D + t_E t_F S = -4 => D = (-4 - t_E t_F S)/2. So D = -2 - (t_E t_F S)/2.

Now the center O = (-D/2, -E/2) = (1 + (t_E t_F S)/4, -E/2). And we have E from D p_x + E p_y = -(t_F+t_E)S => E p_y = -(t_F+t_E)S - D p_x => E = [ -(t_F+t_E)S - D p_x ] / p_y.

Now the line L: through H=(x_H, p_y) with direction P. So param: (x_H + t p_x, p_y + t p_y). For tangency, the quadratic in t from plugging into circle equation must have discriminant zero. The equation is: (x_H + t p_x)^2 + (p_y + t p_y)^2 + D (x_H + t p_x) + E (p_y + t p_y) + F = 0. Expand: (p_x^2+p_y^2) t^2 + 2( x_H p_x + p_y^2 ) t + (x_H^2+p_y^2 + D x_H + E p_y + F) = 0. So discriminant condition: (2( x_H p_x + p_y^2 ))^2 - 4 S ( x_H^2+p_y^2 + D x_H + E p_y + F ) = 0 => (x_H p_x + p_y^2)^2 - S ( x_H^2+p_y^2 + D x_H + E p_y + F ) = 0.

Now we have D, E, F in terms of t_E, t_F, S. So we can substitute.

Let’s denote T = t_E t_F S. Then D = -2 - T/2. Also, E = [ -(t_F+t_E)S - D p_x ] / p_y = [ -(t_F+t_E)S + (2 + T/2) p_x ] / p_y.

Now compute the expression inside: x_H^2+p_y^2 + D x_H + E p_y + F = x_H^2+p_y^2 + D x_H + E p_y + T.

But note that E p_y = -(t_F+t_E)S + (2 + T/2) p_x. So:

x_H^2+p_y^2 + D x_H + E p_y + T = x_H^2+p_y^2 + D x_H + (-(t_F+t_E)S + (2 + T/2) p_x) + T.

So the condition becomes:

(x_H p_x + p_y^2)^2 - S [ x_H^2+p_y^2 + D x_H - (t_F+t_E)S + (2 + T/2) p_x + T ] = 0.

Now we also have that D = -2 - T/2, so D x_H = (-2 - T/2) x_H.

Thus the bracket: x_H^2+p_y^2 -2x_H - (T/2)x_H - (t_F+t_E)S + (2 + T/2) p_x + T.

So the condition is:

(x_H p_x + p_y^2)^2 - S [ x_H^2+p_y^2 -2x_H - (T/2)x_H - (t_F+t_E)S + (2 + T/2) p_x + T ] = 0.

Now the radius squared: R_c^2 = distance from O to B: (x0 - u)^2 + (y0 - v)^2 = ( - v(t_M+t_N) )^2 + ( v(1/2+2 t_M t_N) - v )^2 = v^2 (t_M+t_N)^2 + v^2 (1/2+2 t_M t_N - 1)^2 = v^2 [ (t_M+t_N)^2 + (2 t_M t_N - 1/2)^2 ].

So we need to show that λ satisfies this equation. Now we have an expression for λ in terms of p, u, v, t_M, t_N. But p is the x-coordinate of P, and we also have that P is circumcenter of ACD, so P lies on the circle centered at A with radius? Actually we have A=0, P=(p,0). And we have that C and D are on line MN and on circle centered at P radius p. So we can relate p to u,v,t_M,t_N. Also, we have that M and N are on MN at distances r,R from A. So we have t_M^2 = (r^2/(u^2+v^2)) - 1/4, and similarly for t_N. But we also have that the line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB, so its equation is ux+vy = (u^2+v^2)/2. The foot of perpendicular from A to MN is the midpoint of AB, which is (u/2,v/2). So the distance from A to MN is d = sqrt(u^2+v^2)/2. So the coordinates of M and N are given by:

M = (u/2 - v t_M, v/2 + u t_M), N = (u/2 - v t_N, v/2 + u t_N). And we have that |M| = r, |N| = R. So:

(u/2 - v t_M)^2 + (v/2 + u t_M)^2 = r^2 => (u^2+v^2)/4 + (u^2+v^2) t_M^2 = r^2 => (u^2+v^2)(t_M^2 + 1/4) = r^2 => t_M^2 = r^2/(u^2+v^2) - 1/4.

Similarly, t_N^2 = R^2/(u^2+v^2) - 1/4.

Now we also have that the circle centered at P with radius p intersects MN at C and D. The points C and D are the two points on MN such that |X-P| = p. So they satisfy the quadratic in t: (u^2+v^2) t^2 + 2v p t + (u^2+v^2)/4 - u p = 0. So we have t_C + t_D = -2v p/(u^2+v^2), and t_C t_D = ((u^2+v^2)/4 - u p)/(u^2+v^2) = 1/4 - (u p)/(u^2+v^2).

Now we also have that P is circumcenter of ACD, so PA=PC=PD. That gave us that C and D are on circle centered at P radius p. So that is satisfied.

Now we need to find λ from the orthocenter condition. We have λ = - (A B + C D)/(u B + v D), where A = p - u/2 + v t_M, B = u/2 - v t_N - p, C = -v/2 - u t_M, D = v/2 + u t_N.

We can try to simplify A B + C D and u B + v D. Let's compute:

A = p - u/2 + v t_M.
B = u/2 - v t_N - p = -(p - u/2 + v t_N). So B = - (p - u/2 + v t_N).
C = -v/2 - u t_M.
D = v/2 + u t_N.

Thus A B = - (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N). So A B = - (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N).
C D = (-v/2 - u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) = - (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) because (-v/2 - u t_M) = -(v/2+u t_M). So C D = - (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N).

Thus A B + C D = - [ (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) + (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) ].

Now u B + v D = u * (u/2 - v t_N - p) + v * (v/2 + u t_N) = u(u/2 - v t_N - p) + v(v/2 + u t_N) = u^2/2 - u v t_N - u p + v^2/2 + u v t_N = (u^2+v^2)/2 - u p.

So u B + v D = (u^2+v^2)/2 - u p.

Thus λ = - (A B + C D)/(u B + v D) = - [ - ( (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) + (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) ) ] / ((u^2+v^2)/2 - u p) = ( (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) + (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) ) / ((u^2+v^2)/2 - u p).

Now we need to show that λ satisfies the tangency condition: (1/2+2 t_M t_N - λ)^2 = (t_M+t_N)^2 + (2 t_M t_N - 1/2)^2.

Let's denote X = t_M, Y = t_N for simplicity. Then we have:

λ = ( (p - u/2 + v X)(p - u/2 + v Y) + (v/2 + u X)(v/2 + u Y) ) / ((u^2+v^2)/2 - u p).

Now we also have expressions for p in terms of u,v,X,Y? Since P is circumcenter of ACD, and C and D are on MN with parameters t_C and t_D, which are the roots of the quadratic. But we also have that the midpoint of CD is the foot of perpendicular from P to MN. That foot is the point on MN where the line through P parallel to AB meets MN. Since AB direction is (u,v), the line through P parallel to AB is: (p,0) + t(u,v). Its intersection with MN: ux+vy = (u^2+v^2)/2. So we have u(p + t u) + v(0 + t v) = u p + t(u^2+v^2) = (u^2+v^2)/2 => t = ((u^2+v^2)/2 - u p)/(u^2+v^2) = 1/2 - (u p)/(u^2+v^2). So the foot is at (p + t u, t v) = (p + u(1/2 - u p/(u^2+v^2)), v(1/2 - u p/(u^2+v^2))). This foot is the midpoint of CD. So we have:

t_C + t_D = 2 * (the parameter of the foot along MN? Actually the parameter along MN: The foot corresponds to some t_f such that the point on MN is (u/2 - v t_f, v/2 + u t_f). So we have:

u/2 - v t_f = p + u(1/2 - u p/(u^2+v^2)), and v/2 + u t_f = v(1/2 - u p/(u^2+v^2)). Solve for t_f from the second: v/2 + u t_f = v/2 - v u p/(u^2+v^2) => u t_f = - v u p/(u^2+v^2) => t_f = - v p/(u^2+v^2). So the foot parameter is t_f = - v p/(u^2+v^2). That is consistent with the sum of t_C and t_D: t_C + t_D = -2v p/(u^2+v^2) = 2 t_f. So indeed the midpoint of CD corresponds to t_f = (t_C+t_D)/2. So t_f = - v p/(u^2+v^2). Good.

Now we also have that the foot is the projection of P onto MN. So we have that the vector from P to the foot is perpendicular to MN. Since MN has direction (-v, u), the vector from P to foot is: (u/2 - v t_f - p, v/2 + u t_f - 0) = (u/2 - v(-v p/(u^2+v^2)) - p, v/2 + u(-v p/(u^2+v^2))) = (u/2 + v^2 p/(u^2+v^2) - p, v/2 - u v p/(u^2+v^2)). Simplify: u/2 + p(v^2/(u^2+v^2) - 1) = u/2 - p(u^2/(u^2+v^2)). So the vector is (u/2 - p u^2/(u^2+v^2), v/2 - u v p/(u^2+v^2)). That vector should be parallel to the normal of MN, which is (u,v). Indeed, (u/2 - p u^2/(u^2+v^2), v/2 - u v p/(u^2+v^2)) = (u/2, v/2) - (p u^2/(u^2+v^2), u v p/(u^2+v^2)) = (u/2, v/2) - (p u/(u^2+v^2)) (u, v). So it's (u/2, v/2) - (p u/(u^2+v^2)) (u, v). That is indeed parallel to (u,v) because (u/2, v/2) is a multiple of (u,v) and the second term is also a multiple. So it's consistent.

Now we have t_f = - v p/(u^2+v^2). So p = - (u^2+v^2) t_f / v.

Now also we have that M and N have parameters t_M and t_N. And we have that the distance from A to MN is d = sqrt(u^2+v^2)/2. So we have t_M^2 = r^2/(u^2+v^2) - 1/4. Similarly, (u^2+v^2)(t_N^2 + 1/4) = R^2.

Their product = 0.959*0.149 = 0.143. Now v/2 + u t_M = -0.81 + 1.172*0.5 = -0.81+0.586 = -0.224. v/2 + u t_N = -0.81 + 1.172*1 = -0.81+1.172=0.362. Their product = -0.224*0.362 = -0.081. So numerator = 0.143 -0.081 = 0.062. Denominator: (u^2+v^2)/2 = (1.172^2+1.62^2)/2 = (1.374+2.624)/2=3.998/2=1.999. u p = 1.172*2.355=2.759. So denominator = 1.999-2.759 = -0.76. So λ = 0.062/(-0.76) = -0.0816. So λ ≈ -0.0816. Then y_H = λ v = (-0.0816)*(-1.62)=0.132. So the horizontal line is y=0.132. Now the circle through B, E, F: B=(1.172,-1.62). E = (u-2v t_M, 0) = (1.172 - 2*(-1.62)*0.5, 0) = (1.172 + 1.62, 0) = (2.792, 0). F = (u-2v t_N, 0) = (1.172 - 2*(-1.62)*1, 0) = (1.172 + 3.24, 0) = (4.412, 0). So circle through (1.172,-1.62), (2.792,0), (4.412,0). The center O: x0 = (2.792+4.412)/2 = 3.602. y0 = v(1/2+2 t_M t_N) = (-1.62)*(0.5+2*0.5*1) = -1.62*(0.5+1)= -1.62*1.5= -2.43. So O = (3.602, -2.43). Radius R_c = distance to B: (3.602-1.172)^2+(-2.43+1.62)^2 = (2.43)^2+(-0.81)^2 = 5.9049+0.6561=6.561, sqrt=2.561. The horizontal line y=0.132 is tangent? The distance from O to y=0.132 is | -2.43 - 0.132| = 2.562. That equals R_c. So indeed tangency holds. So the condition is satisfied.

Thus we have a clear synthetic interpretation: In the coordinate system where AP is the x-axis, we have that the line through H parallel to AP (horizontal) is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if |y0 - λ v| = R_c, which we derived to be equivalent to (1/2+2 t_M t_N - λ)^2 = (t_M+t_N)^2 + (2 t_M t_N - 1/2)^2. And we found that λ = ( (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) + (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) ) / ((u^2+v^2)/2 - u p). Now we also have that p = - (u^2+v^2) t_f / v, where t_f = (t_C+t_D)/2 = - v p/(u^2+v^2). And we have that t_M and t_N are related to r,R and u,v. But we can also find that t_M and t_N are such that the points M and N are on MN with parameters t_M and t_N. And we have that the circle centered at P with radius p passes through A and C and D. So we have that the foot of perpendicular from P to MN is at t_f. That gives a relation between p and t_f. Also, we have that M and N are points on MN at distances r,R from A. So we have t_M^2 = r^2/(u^2+v^2) - 1/4, and similarly for t_N. But we also have that the line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB, so its equation is ux+vy = (u^2+v^2)/2. Given the complexity, I think the analytic proof is the most straightforward. We can present it as follows: Set up coordinate system with A at origin and AP as the x-axis. Then we can compute everything in terms of the parameters u,v, t_M, t_N, and p. Then we can show that the orthocenter H of triangle PMN has y-coordinate λ v, and that λ satisfies the equation (1/2+2 t_M t_N - λ)^2 = (t_M+t_N)^2 + (2 t_M t_N - 1/2)^2, which is equivalent to the tangency condition. Then we can verify that λ indeed satisfies that equation using the relations that come from the fact that M and N lie on the perpendicular bisector of AB and that P is the circumcenter of ACD. This verification can be done algebraically.

Given the time, I'll produce a rigorous analytic proof. I'll set up coordinates with A=(0,0), and let the line AP be the x-axis. So P=(p,0) with p>0. Let B=(u,v) with v≠0. Then the circles Ω and Γ pass through A and B. Their centers M and N lie on the perpendicular bisector of AB. The perpendicular bisector of AB is the line: ux+vy = (u^2+v^2)/2. So M and N are points on this line. Let their coordinates be M=(x_M,y_M) and N=(x_N,y_N). Since they lie on the line, we can parameterize them as:

M = (u/2 - v t_M, v/2 + u t_M) for some real t_M.
N = (u/2 - v t_N, v/2 + u t_N) for some real t_N.

The radii are: r = |M| = sqrt( (u/2 - v t_M)^2 + (v/2 + u t_M)^2 ) = sqrt( (u^2+v^2)/4 + (u^2+v^2) t_M^2 ) = sqrt( (u^2+v^2)(1/4 + t_M^2) ). Similarly, R = sqrt( (u^2+v^2)(1/4 + t_N^2) ).

Now, the line MN is the perpendicular bisector, so its direction vector is (-v, u). The points C and D are the intersections of this line with the circumcircle of triangle ACD. But we know that P is the circumcenter of triangle ACD, so PA=PC=PD. Since A=(0,0), PA = p. So C and D lie on the circle centered at P with radius p. So they are the two points where the line MN (parameterized by t) meets the circle (X-P)^2 = p^2. Substituting X = (u/2 - v t, v/2 + u t), we get:

(u/2 - v t - p)^2 + (v/2 + u t)^2 = p^2.

Expanding: (u/2 - p)^2 - 2(u/2 - p)v t + v^2 t^2 + (v/2)^2 + 2(v/2)u t + u^2 t^2 = p^2.

Combine: (u^2+v^2)/4 + (u^2+v^2) t^2 + [ -2(u/2 - p)v + u v ] t = p^2.

Simplify linear term: -2(u/2 - p)v + u v = -u v + 2p v + u v = 2p v. So we have:

(u^2+v^2)(t^2 + 1/4) + 2p v t = p^2.

Thus (u^2+v^2) t^2 + 2p v t + (u^2+v^2)/4 - p^2 = 0.

So the two solutions t_C and t_D are the parameters of C and D. Their sum and product:

t_C + t_D = -2p v/(u^2+v^2), t_C t_D = ((u^2+v^2)/4 - p^2)/(u^2+v^2) = 1/4 - p^2/(u^2+v^2).

Now, we also have that the orthocenter H of triangle PMN. Since P is on x-axis, and M,N are on line MN, the altitude from P is perpendicular to MN. Since MN has direction (-v, u), a perpendicular direction is (u, v). So the altitude from P is the line through P with direction (u, v). So H lies on this line: H = (p + λ u, λ v) for some λ. Also, H lies on the altitude from M, which is perpendicular to PN. So (H-M)·PN = 0. Compute PN = N - P = (u/2 - v t_N - p, v/2 + u t_N - 0) = (u/2 - v t_N - p, v/2 + u t_N). And M = (u/2 - v t_M, v/2 + u t_M). So H-M = (p + λ u - (u/2 - v t_M), λ v - (v/2 + u t_M)) = (p - u/2 + v t_M + λ u, -v/2 + λ v - u t_M). Then dot with PN:

(p - u/2 + v t_M + λ u)(u/2 - v t_N - p) + (-v/2 + λ v - u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) = 0.

Let’s denote A = p - u/2 + v t_M, B = u/2 - v t_N - p, C = -v/2 - u t_M, D = v/2 + u t_N. Then condition: (A + λ u) B + (C + λ v) D = 0 => A B + λ u B + C D + λ v D = 0 => λ (u B + v D) = - (A B + C D). So λ = - (A B + C D)/(u B + v D).

Now compute u B + v D = u(u/2 - v t_N - p) + v(v/2 + u t_N) = u^2/2 - u v t_N - u p + v^2/2 + u v t_N = (u^2+v^2)/2 - u p.

Also, A B + C D = (p - u/2 + v t_M)(u/2 - v t_N - p) + (-v/2 - u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N). Note that (u/2 - v t_N - p) = -(p - u/2 + v t_N). So A B = - (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N). And C D = (-v/2 - u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) = - (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N). Thus A B + C D = -[ (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) + (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) ].

Therefore, λ = - ( -[ (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) + (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) ] ) / ((u^2+v^2)/2 - u p) = [ (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) + (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) ] / ((u^2+v^2)/2 - u p).

And |M-B|^2 = (u/2 - v t - u)^2 + (v/2 + u t - v)^2 = (-u/2 - v t)^2 + (-v/2 + u t)^2 = (u/2 + v t)^2 + (v/2 - u t)^2 = (u^2+v^2)/4 + (u^2+v^2)t^2. So indeed |M| = |M-B|. So the parameterization ensures that M is equidistant from A and B, so it's on the perpendicular bisector. So that's fine.

Thus O_y = v (1+4 t_M t_N)/2.

B = w, E and F are the second intersections of the real axis (line through A and P) with circles Ω and Γ. Since A=0 is on both circles, the other intersections are given by the chord through A. For circle Ω: center M = w/2 + t_M i w, radius r = |M|. The line through A and P is the real axis. So we need to find the point on real axis other than 0 that lies on Ω. The condition for X real: |X - M|^2 = r^2. Since |M|^2 = r^2, we have X^2 - 2X Re(M) + |M|^2 = r^2 => X^2 - 2X Re(M) = 0 => X(X-2 Re(M))=0 => X=0 or X=2 Re(M). So E = 2 Re(M). Similarly, F = 2 Re(N). So E and F are real numbers.

Thus the circle (BEF) passes through B=w, E=2 Re(M), F=2 Re(N). Since E and F are real, the circle's center O lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF, which is the vertical line x = (E+F)/2 = Re(M)+Re(N). So O_x = Re(M)+Re(N). Also, the circle equation can be written as: (x - O_x)^2 + (y - O_y)^2 = R^2. The horizontal line through H is y = Im(H) = λ Im(w) (since H = p + λ w, so Im(H)=λ v). For tangency, we need |O_y - λ v| = R. So we need to show that the distance from the center to the horizontal line equals the radius.

Now we can compute O_y and R in terms of w and t_M,t_N. We have:

Re(M) = Re( w/2 + t_M i w ) = (u/2) + t_M Re(i w) = u/2 + t_M (-v) = u/2 - v t_M.
Similarly, Re(N) = u/2 - v t_N.
So E = u - 2v t_M, F = u - 2v t_N. Then O_x = (E+F)/2 = u - v(t_M+t_N).

Now O_y can be found using the fact that O lies on the perpendicular bisector of BE. The midpoint of B and E is ((u+E)/2, v/2) = ((u+u-2v t_M)/2, v/2) = (u - v t_M, v/2). The slope of BE is (v-0)/(u - (u-2v t_M)) = v/(2v t_M) = 1/(2 t_M). So the perpendicular slope is -2 t_M. So the equation of perpendicular bisector of BE: y - v/2 = -2 t_M (x - (u - v t_M)). Since O lies on this line and also on x = O_x = u - v(t_M+t_N), we can find O_y:

O_y - v/2 = -2 t_M ( (u - v(t_M+t_N)) - (u - v t_M) ) = -2 t_M ( - v t_N ) = 2 v t_M t_N.
Thus O_y = v/2 + 2 v t_M t_N = v (1/2 + 2 t_M t_N). So O_y = v (1+4 t_M t_N)/2, as before.

Now R_c = distance from O to B: (O_x - u)^2 + (O_y - v)^2 = ( - v(t_M+t_N) )^2 + ( v(1+4 t_M t_N)/2 - v )^2 = v^2 (t_M+t_N)^2 + v^2 ( (1+4 t_M t_N)/2 - 1 )^2 = v^2 (t_M+t_N)^2 + v^2 ( (1+4 t_M t_N - 2)/2 )^2 = v^2 (t_M+t_N)^2 + v^2 ( (4 t_M t_N -1)/2 )^2 = v^2 [ (t_M+t_N)^2 + (4 t_M t_N -1)^2/4 ].

Now the condition for tangency is: |O_y - λ v| = R_c. Since O_y = v (1+4 t_M t_N)/2, we have O_y - λ v = v (1+4 t_M t_N)/2 - λ v = v ( (1+4 t_M t_N)/2 - λ ). So condition becomes:

v^2 ( (1+4 t_M t_N)/2 - λ )^2 = v^2 [ (t_M+t_N)^2 + (4 t_M t_N -1)^2/4 ].

Cancel v^2 (v≠0): ( (1+4 t_M t_N)/2 - λ )^2 = (t_M+t_N)^2 + (4 t_M t_N -1)^2/4.

Compute right side: (t_M+t_N)^2 + (4 t_M t_N -1)^2/4 = (t_M^2+2 t_M t_N + t_N^2) + (16 t_M^2 t_N^2 -8 t_M t_N +1)/4 = t_M^2+t_N^2+2 t_M t_N + 4 t_M^2 t_N^2 -2 t_M t_N + 1/4 = t_M^2+t_N^2 + 4 t_M^2 t_N^2 + 1/4.

Left side: ( (1+4 t_M t_N)/2 - λ )^2 = ( (1+4 t_M t_N)/2 )^2 - λ(1+4 t_M t_N) + λ^2 = (1+8 t_M t_N+16 t_M^2 t_N^2)/4 - λ(1+4 t_M t_N) + λ^2 = 1/4 + 2 t_M t_N + 4 t_M^2 t_N^2 - λ(1+4 t_M t_N) + λ^2.

Thus equation becomes:

1/4 + 2 t_M t_N + 4 t_M^2 t_N^2 - λ(1+4 t_M t_N) + λ^2 = t_M^2+t_N^2 + 4 t_M^2 t_N^2 + 1/4.

Cancel 1/4 and 4 t_M^2 t_N^2: 2 t_M t_N - λ(1+4 t_M t_N) + λ^2 = t_M^2+t_N^2.

Rearrange: λ^2 - λ(1+4 t_M t_N) + 2 t_M t_N - (t_M^2+t_N^2) = 0 => λ^2 - λ(1+4 t_M t_N) - (t_M^2 -2 t_M t_N + t_N^2) = 0 => λ^2 - λ(1+4 t_M t_N) - (t_M - t_N)^2 = 0. So λ must satisfy this quadratic.

Now we need to show that the λ given by the orthocenter condition satisfies this quadratic. We have an expression for λ. Let's compute λ in terms of t_M,t_N,u,v,p. We have:

λ = [ (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) + (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) ] / ((u^2+v^2)/2 - u p).

Now we can also use the fact that p is related to t_f, but we can also find that (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) = (p - u/2)^2 + v(p - u/2)(t_M+t_N) + v^2 t_M t_N. And the second term is v^2/4 + u v (t_M+t_N)/2 + u^2 t_M t_N. So numerator = (p - u/2)^2 + v(p - u/2)(t_M+t_N) + v^2 t_M t_N + v^2/4 + u v (t_M+t_N)/2 + u^2 t_M t_N.

Denominator = (u^2+v^2)/2 - u p.

Now we also have that the points C and D are the intersections of line MN with circle centered at P. So t_C and t_D are roots of the quadratic: (u^2+v^2) t^2 + 2p v t + (u^2+v^2)/4 - p^2 = 0. So we have:

t_C + t_D = -2p v/(u^2+v^2), t_C t_D = ((u^2+v^2)/4 - p^2)/(u^2+v^2) = 1/4 - p^2/(u^2+v^2).

Now, we also have that the points M and N are on MN. So we can compute the power of M with respect to circle (P,p) as we did: |M-P|^2 - p^2 = (u^2+v^2)/4 - u p + 2p v t_M + (u^2+v^2) t_M^2. This is equal to (u^2+v^2) (t_C - t_M)(t_D - t_M). So we have:

(u^2+v^2) (t_C - t_M)(t_D - t_M) = (u^2+v^2)/4 - u p + 2p v t_M + (u^2+v^2) t_M^2. (10)

Similarly, for N: (u^2+v^2) (t_C - t_N)(t_D - t_N) = (u^2+v^2)/4 - u p + 2p v t_N + (u^2+v^2) t_N^2. (11)

Now, we also have that the orthocenter condition gave λ = [ (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) + (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N) ] / ((u^2+v^2)/2 - u p). We can try to express the numerator in terms of (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) and (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N). Perhaps we can relate these to the power expressions.

Note that (p - u/2 + v t_M) = (p - u/2) + v t_M. And (v/2 + u t_M) = v/2 + u t_M. So the numerator is the sum of two products. We can try to compute the product (p - u/2 + v t_M)(p - u/2 + v t_N) + (v/2 + u t_M)(v/2 + u t_N). This is reminiscent of the expression for the sum of two products. Maybe we can find that this sum equals something like (u^2+v^2)/2 - u p times something? Actually we have denominator = (u^2+v^2)/2 - u p. So if we can show that numerator = ( (u^2+v^2)/2 - u p ) * (something), then λ = that something. And we need to show that something satisfies the quadratic. So we need to find an expression for λ that is simpler.

Midpoint of AC: ((h - r)/2, k/2). Slope of AC: (k - 0)/(h - (-r)) = k/(h+r). So slope of perpendicular bisector: -(h+r)/k. Equation: y - k/2 = -(h+r)/k (x - (h - r)/2). Since P lies on x = (d+R - r)/2, we can plug to find y-coordinate.

Let x_P = (d+R - r)/2.

Then y_P = k/2 - (h+r)/k * (x_P - (h - r)/2).

Compute x_P - (h - r)/2 = (d+R - r)/2 - (h - r)/2 = (d+R - r - h + r)/2 = (d + R - h)/2.

So y_P = k/2 - (h+r)/k * ((d+R - h)/2) = k/2 - (h+r)(d+R - h)/(2k).

Thus P = ( (d+R - r)/2 , k/2 - (h+r)(d+R - h)/(2k) ).

Simplify: y_P = (k^2 - (h+r)(d+R - h)) / (2k). Since k^2 = r^2 - h^2.

So y_P = (r^2 - h^2 - (h+r)(d+R - h)) / (2k).

Compute (h+r)(d+R - h) = (h+r)(d+R) - (h+r)h = (h+r)(d+R) - h(h+r) = (h+r)(d+R - h). So y_P = (r^2 - h^2 - (h+r)(d+R - h)) / (2k). Expand: r^2 - h^2 - (h+r)(d+R - h) = r^2 - h^2 - (h+r)(d+R) + (h+r)h = r^2 - h^2 - (h+r)(d+R) + h^2 + rh = r^2 + rh - (h+r)(d+R) = r(r+h) - (h+r)(d+R) = (h+r)(r - (d+R)). But we can use chord properties: If we have line through A with direction vector v, then the second intersection is given by A + t v where t satisfies that the point is on circle. But careful: For circle centered at origin, equation x^2+y^2=r^2. We can compute the circumcircle by using the condition that the points satisfy a circle equation: x^2+y^2 + Ax + By + C = 0. Since A=0 is not necessarily on the circle, but we can plug B, E, F.

Let’s do that: Let circle equation: x^2+y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0. For point B=(2,0): 4 + 2D + F = 0 => 2D + F = -4. (1)
For E=(λ p_x, λ p_y): λ^2(p_x^2+p_y^2) + D λ p_x + E λ p_y + F = 0 => λ^2 P·P + λ (D p_x + E p_y) + F = 0. (2)
For F=(μ p_x, μ p_y): μ^2 P·P + μ (D p_x + E p_y) + F = 0. (3)

We have three equations in D, E, F. Subtract (2) and (3) to eliminate F: (μ^2 - λ^2)P·P + (μ - λ)(D p_x + E p_y) = 0 => (μ - λ)[(μ+λ)P·P + (D p_x+E p_y)] = 0. Since μ ≠ λ (E and F distinct), we have (μ+λ)P·P + (D p_x+E p_y) = 0 => D p_x+E p_y = -(μ+λ)P·P. (4)

Now from (2): λ^2 P·P + λ (D p_x+E p_y) + F = 0 => λ^2 P·P + λ (-(μ+λ)P·P) + F = 0 => λ^2 P·P - λμ P·P - λ^2 P·P + F = 0 => -λμ P·P + F = 0 => F = λμ P·P. (5)

Now from (1): 2D + F = -4 => 2D + λμ P·P = -4 => D = (-4 - λμ P·P)/2. (6)

Now we also have (4): D p_x+E p_y = -(μ+λ)P·P. So we can solve for E once D known: E p_y = -(μ+λ)P·P - D p_x. So E = [-(μ+λ)P·P - D p_x]/p_y, provided p_y ≠ 0. So we have the circle equation.

Now the center O = (-D/2, -E/2). So O = ( -D/2, -E/2 ). And radius squared = O^2 - F? Actually for circle x^2+y^2+Dx+Ey+F=0, center = (-D/2, -E/2), radius^2 = (D^2+E^2)/4 - F. So we can compute.

The distance from point O to line L: line L has direction vector P = (p_x, p_y). So a normal vector to L is perpendicular to P. For line L: (x - x_H, y - y_P) cross P = 0? Actually line L: param eq. The distance from O to L = |(O - H) × P| / |P|, where × denotes 2D cross product (determinant). So we need to show that |(O - H) × P| / |P| = radius.

Alternatively, we can check that the line L is tangent if and only if the equation for intersection (substituting L into circle equation) has a double root. That is, the quadratic in t from plugging (x_H + t p_x, y_P + t p_y) into circle equation has discriminant zero. That might be simpler: we have circle equation: x^2+y^2+Dx+Ey+F=0. For tangency, discriminant = 0: [2( x_H p_x + y_P p_y )]^2 - 4 (p_x^2+p_y^2) (x_H^2+y_P^2 + D x_H + E y_P + F) = 0 => (x_H p_x + y_P p_y)^2 - (p_x^2+p_y^2)(x_H^2+y_P^2 + D x_H + E y_P + F) = 0.

So we need to verify that this holds given our expressions.

Now define:

y_C = m - r, y_D = n + R. So C = (1, y_C), D = (1, y_D).

Now P = (x_P, y_P) where:

y_P = (y_C + y_D)/2 = (m - r + n + R)/2 = (m + n + R - r)/2.

Now we also have r = sqrt(1+m^2), R = sqrt(1+n^2). So these are functions of m,n.

Similarly μ = 2 (P·N)/(P·P) with N=(1,n): P·N = x_P + n y_P.

And P·P = x_P^2 + y_P^2.

Now E = λ P, F = μ P.

Now B = (2,0). So we have circle through B, E, F.

Thus (m - y_P)(n - y_P) = ((m - n + r - R)/2)*((n - m + r - R)/2) = (( (r-R) + (m-n) ) * ((r-R) - (m-n) ) /4? Actually (m-n) = -(n-m). So let’s set Δ = n-m >0. Then m-n = -Δ. So m - y_P = (-Δ + r - R)/2 = (r - R - Δ)/2. n - y_P = (Δ + r - R)/2 = (r - R + Δ)/2. So product = ((r-R)^2 - Δ^2)/4.

Thus (m - y_P)(n - y_P) = ((r-R)^2 - Δ^2)/4.

Now y_C y_D = (m - r)(n+R) = (m - r)(n+R). Expand: (m - r)(n+R) = m n + m R - r n - r R. So 1 + y_C y_D = 1 + m n + m R - r n - r R.

Now we also have S = x_P^2 + y_P^2. Let's compute x_P and y_P in terms of m,n,r,R.

x_P = (1 - y_C y_D)/2 = (1 - (m - r)(n+R))/2.
y_P = (m - r + n+R)/2 = (m+n+R-r)/2.

We can also compute P·M = x_P + m y_P = (1 - (m - r)(n+R))/2 + m*(m+n+R-r)/2 = [1 - (m - r)(n+R) + m(m+n+R-r)]/2.
Similarly P·N = [1 - (m - r)(n+R) + n(m+n+R-r)]/2.

Now compute these expressions. Let's expand:

First, compute (m - r)(n+R) = m n + m R - r n - r R.
Then 1 - that = 1 - m n - m R + r n + r R.

Now m(m+n+R-r) = m^2 + m n + m R - m r.
So numerator for P·M: (1 - m n - m R + r n + r R) + (m^2 + m n + m R - m r) = 1 - m n - m R + r n + r R + m^2 + m n + m R - m r = 1 + m^2 + r n + r R - m r.

Thus P·M = (1 + m^2 + r n + r R - m r)/2 = (r^2 + r n + r R - m r)/2? Since 1+m^2 = r^2. So P·M = (r^2 + r(n+R - m))/2 = r (r + n+R - m)/2 = r (r + n+R - m)/2.

But note that y_P = (m+n+R-r)/2 = (n+R + m - r)/2. So r + n+R - m = r + (n+R - m). That is not directly y_P. However we can write P·M = r (r + n+R - m)/2.

Similarly, P·N = (1 - (m - r)(n+R) + n(m+n+R-r))/2 = [1 - m n - m R + r n + r R + n m + n^2 + n R - n r]/2 = [1 - m R + r n + r R + n^2 + n R - n r]/2 = [1 + n^2 + r n - m R + r R + n R - n r]/2? Actually r n - n r = 0. So P·N = (1 + n^2 + r R + n R - m R)/2 = (R^2 + R(r+n) - m R)/2? Since 1+n^2 = R^2. So P·N = (R^2 + R(r+n) - m R)/2 = R (R + r+n - m)/2 = R (R + n + r - m)/2.

So we have:

P·M = (r/2)(r + n + R - m)
P·N = (R/2)(R + n + r - m)

Now note that r + n + R - m = (r - m) + (n+R). But we also have y_P = (m+n+R-r)/2 = (n+R + m - r)/2. So r + n+R - m = (r - m) + (n+R) = (n+R) - (m - r). That is not directly y_P. But we can also write r + n+R - m = (r - m) + (n+R) = -(m-r) + (n+R). So it's (n+R) - (m-r). And (m-r) = -y_C? Actually y_C = m-r, so m-r = y_C. So r + n+R - m = (n+R) - y_C. And y_P = (y_C + y_D)/2 = (y_C + (n+R))/2. So (n+R) - y_C = 2y_P - 2y_C? Actually y_P = (y_C + y_D)/2, and y_D = n+R, so y_P = (y_C + (n+R))/2 => 2y_P = y_C + n+R => n+R - y_C = 2y_P - 2y_C? No: n+R - y_C = 2y_P - 2y_C? Because 2y_P = y_C + n+R => n+R = 2y_P - y_C => n+R - y_C = 2y_P - 2y_C. So r + n+R - m = (n+R) - y_C = 2y_P - 2y_C. So P·M = (r/2)*(2y_P - 2y_C) = r (y_P - y_C). So P·M = r (y_P - y_C). Similarly, P·N = R (y_P - y_D)? Let's check: R + n + r - m = (R + n) + (r - m) = (n+R) + (r-m) = y_D + y_C? Actually y_D = n+R, y_C = m-r = -(r-m). So r-m = -y_C. So R + n + r - m = y_D - y_C. And y_P = (y_C+y_D)/2 => 2y_P = y_C+y_D => y_D - y_C = 2y_P - 2y_C? Actually y_D - y_C = (2y_P - y_C) - y_C = 2y_P - 2y_C. So P·N = (R/2)*(y_D - y_C) = R (y_P - y_C)? Wait y_D - y_C = 2y_P - 2y_C, so (R/2)*(2y_P - 2y_C) = R(y_P - y_C). So P·N = R(y_P - y_C). But note that y_C is negative, y_P maybe positive? So P·N = R(y_P - y_C) = R(y_P - (m-r)). But we also have y_D = n+R, so y_P - y_D = (y_C+y_D)/2 - y_D = (y_C - y_D)/2 = -(y_D - y_C)/2 = -(2y_P - 2y_C)/2 = -(y_P - y_C). So y_P - y_D = -(y_P - y_C). So P·N = R(y_P - y_C) = -R(y_P - y_D). So P·N = -R(y_P - y_D). But we also have P·M = r(y_P - y_C). So both P·M and P·N are expressed in terms of y_P - y_C.

Now we also have S = x_P^2 + y_P^2. We can compute S in terms of y_C, y_D, etc. But maybe we can find a relation between P·M, P·N, and S. Since λ = 2(P·M)/S, μ = 2(P·N)/S. So λ and μ are positive if P·M and P·N positive. We'll see.

Now we also need O_x = 1 + (P·M)(P·N)/S. And O_y = (P·M+P·N - p_x - p_x (P·M)(P·N)/S)/p_y.

Now we need to compute the distance from O to L. Let's compute H coordinates more simply.

We have H = (x_H, y_P) with x_H = 1 + (m - y_P)(n - y_P)/(1 - x_P). And we have expressions for m - y_P and n - y_P. Let's compute them in terms of y_C, y_D, etc.

We have y_P = (y_C + y_D)/2. So m - y_P = m - (y_C+y_D)/2. But m =? y_C = m - r => m = y_C + r. So m - y_P = y_C + r - (y_C+y_D)/2 = (2y_C + 2r - y_C - y_D)/2 = (y_C + 2r - y_D)/2. Similarly n - y_P = n - (y_C+y_D)/2 = (2n - y_C - y_D)/2. But n = y_D - R? Actually y_D = n+R => n = y_D - R. So n - y_P = y_D - R - (y_C+y_D)/2 = (2y_D - 2R - y_C - y_D)/2 = (y_D - y_C - 2R)/2. So m - y_P = (y_C + 2r - y_D)/2, n - y_P = (y_D - y_C - 2R)/2.

Now product (m - y_P)(n - y_P) = ((y_C + 2r - y_D)(y_D - y_C - 2R))/4. But earlier we had (m - y_P)(n - y_P) = ((r-R)^2 - Δ^2)/4. Let's keep that.

Now 1 - x_P = (1 + y_C y_D)/2. So x_H = 1 + (m - y_P)(n - y_P) / ((1 + y_C y_D)/2) = 1 + 2(m - y_P)(n - y_P)/(1 + y_C y_D) = 1 + 2 * ((r-R)^2 - Δ^2)/4 / (1 + y_C y_D) = 1 + ((r-R)^2 - Δ^2)/(2(1 + y_C y_D)).

Thus x_H = 1 + ((r-R)^2 - Δ^2) / (2(1 + y_C y_D)).

Now we also have y_P = (m+n+R-r)/2 = (m+n+R-r)/2. But we might also express y_P in terms of r,R,Δ? Since m =? We have y_C = m - r, so m = y_C + r. y_D = n+R, so n = y_D - R. Then m+n = y_C + r + y_D - R = (y_C+y_D) + (r-R). So m+n+R-r = (y_C+y_D)+(r-R)+R-r = y_C+y_D. So y_P = (y_C+y_D)/2, consistent. So y_P = (y_C+y_D)/2.

Now we need to compute the discriminant condition: (x_H p_x + y_P p_y)^2 - S (x_H^2+y_P^2 + D x_H + E y_P + F) = 0.

But we have expressions for D, E, F in terms of P·M, P·N, S, p_x, p_y. Also O_x = 1 + (P·M)(P·N)/S. And O_y = (P·M+P·N - p_x - p_x (P·M)(P·N)/S)/p_y.

We also have that the power of H with respect to circle is: Pow(H) = (H_x^2+H_y^2) + D H_x + E H_y + F. But we can also compute Pow(H) = (H - O)^2 - R_c^2. And the distance from O to L is |(O - H) × P|/|P|. For tangency, we need that distance equals radius, i.e., |(O - H) × P|/|P| = R_c. This is equivalent to (O - H) × P = ± R_c |P|. But also we can square: |(O - H) × P|^2 = R_c^2 S. And note that |(O - H) × P|^2 = |O - H|^2 S - ((O - H)·P)^2. So condition becomes |O - H|^2 S - ((O - H)·P)^2 = R_c^2 S => |O - H|^2 - ((O - H)·P)^2/S = R_c^2. But R_c^2 = |O - H|^2 - Pow(H)? Actually Pow(H) = |H - O|^2 - R_c^2, so R_c^2 = |H - O|^2 - Pow(H). So condition becomes |H - O|^2 - ((O - H)·P)^2/S = |H - O|^2 - Pow(H) => ((O - H)·P)^2/S = Pow(H). So ( (O - H)·P )^2 = S Pow(H). That is a condition that the projection of (O-H) onto P has magnitude sqrt(S Pow(H)). But we also have that the line L is tangent if and only if the distance from O to L equals radius. The distance from O to L is |(O - H) × P|/|P|. And we have that |(O - H) × P|^2 = |O - H|^2 S - ((O - H)·P)^2. So condition is |O - H|^2 S - ((O - H)·P)^2 = R_c^2 S = (|O - H|^2 - Pow(H)) S => |O - H|^2 S - ((O - H)·P)^2 = |O - H|^2 S - Pow(H) S => ((O - H)·P)^2 = Pow(H) S. So indeed the tangency condition is equivalent to ((O - H)·P)^2 = S Pow(H). This is a scalar condition.

Now we can compute (O - H)·P = O·P - H·P. And O·P =? O = (O_x, O_y). P = (p_x, p_y). So O·P = O_x p_x + O_y p_y. And H·P = x_H p_x + y_P p_y.

So condition: (O_x p_x + O_y p_y - x_H p_x - y_P p_y)^2 = S (x_H^2+y_P^2 + D x_H + E y_P + F).

But note that D, E, F are expressed in terms of P·M, P·N, S, p_x, p_y. And O_x, O_y are also expressed in terms of those. So maybe we can simplify.

Alternatively, we can use the fact that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF) if and only if the point H lies on the polar of A with respect to that circle? Given the algebraic complexity, perhaps we can choose a different coordinate system that simplifies the algebra. Since the problem involves circles intersecting, we can use inversion about A that sends circles to lines. Let's try that.

Alternatively, we can use complex numbers with A=0 and B=1? Actually we can set A=0, B=1 (by scaling and rotation). Then the circles Ω and Γ are circles through 0 and 1. But maybe we can use vector geometry with coordinates and simplify by using the fact that we have A=0, B=2? Actually we set c=1 earlier, but we can also set AB=2? Let's redo with A=0, B=1? Let's compute some specific case to see the geometry. Choose convenient numbers: Let M=(0,0)? Actually we set A=0, B=2? Let's pick a concrete example to get intuition.

Let’s set A=0, B=2. Then c=1. Choose m=1, then r = sqrt(1+1)=√2≈1.414. So M=(1,1). Choose n=2, then R = sqrt(1+4)=√5≈2.236. So N=(1,2). Then C = (1, m - r) = (1, 1-1.414)= (1, -0.414). D = (1, n+R) = (1, 2+2.236)= (1, 4.236). Then triangle ACD: A(0,0), C(1,-0.414), D(1,4.236). Compute P: x_P = 1/2 - (y_C y_D)/2 = 0.5 - ((-0.414)*4.236)/2 = 0.5 - (-1.753)/2 = 0.5 + 0.8765 = 1.3765. y_P = (y_C+y_D)/2 = (-0.414+4.236)/2 = 3.822/2 = 1.911. So P≈(1.3765,1.911). Then line AP: through origin and P, so direction (1.3765,1.911). Compute E and F: λ = 2(P·M)/S, μ = 2(P·N)/S. Compute P·M = 1.3765*1 + 1.911*1 = 1.3765+1.911=3.2875. S = 1.3765^2+1.911^2 = 1.894+3.652=5.546. So λ = 2*3.2875/5.546 = 6.575/5.546≈1.184. So E = λP = (1.3765*1.184, 1.911*1.184) = (1.629, 2.262). μ = 2(P·N)/S = 2*(1.3765*1+1.911*2)/5.546 = 2*(1.3765+3.822)/5.546 = 2*5.1985/5.546 = 10.397/5.546≈1.873. So F = μP = (1.3765*1.873, 1.911*1.873) = (2.577, 3.579). B=(2,0). So triangle BEF: B(2,0), E(1.629,2.262), F(2.577,3.579). Compute circumcenter O of BEF. We can compute using perpendicular bisectors. Midpoint of BE: ((2+1.629)/2=1.8145, (0+2.262)/2=1.131). Slope of BE: (2.262-0)/(1.629-2) = 2.262/(-0.371) = -6.098. Perpendicular slope = 1/6.098≈0.164. Equation: y-1.131 = 0.164(x-1.8145). Midpoint of BF: ((2+2.577)/2=2.2885, (0+3.579)/2=1.7895). Slope of BF: (3.579-0)/(2.577-2)=3.579/0.577=6.204. Perpendicular slope = -1/6.204≈-0.161. Equation: y-1.7895 = -0.161(x-2.2885). Solve for intersection. From first: y = 1.131 + 0.164(x-1.8145) = 1.131 + 0.164x - 0.297 = 0.834 + 0.164x. From second: y = 1.7895 -0.161(x-2.2885) = 1.7895 -0.161x + 0.368 = 2.1575 -0.161x. Set equal: 0.834+0.164x = 2.1575-0.161x => 0.164x+0.161x = 2.1575-0.834 => 0.325x = 1.3235 => x = 4.074. Then y = 0.834+0.164*4.074 = 0.834+0.668=1.502. So O≈(4.074,1.502). Radius squared: distance from O to B: (4.074-2)^2+(1.502-0)^2 = (2.074)^2+1.502^2 = 4.301+2.256=6.557. So radius≈2.56.

Now compute H: orthocenter of triangle PMN. M=(1,1), N=(1,2), P=(1.3765,1.911). Since MN vertical, altitude from P is horizontal: y=y_P=1.911. Altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. PN = N-P = (1-1.3765, 2-1.911)=(-0.3765,0.089). So direction of PN = (-0.3765,0.089). A perpendicular direction = (0.089,0.3765) (since dot = -0.3765*0.089+0.089*0.3765=0). So altitude from M: through M(1,1) with direction (0.089,0.3765). Param: (1,1)+s(0.089,0.3765). Intersection with y=1.911 gives: 1+0.3765s = 1.911 => s = 0.5/0.3765? Actually 1.911-1=0.911, so s = 0.911/0.3765 ≈ 2.419. Then x_H = 1 + 0.089*2.419 = 1 + 0.215 = 1.215. So H≈(1.215,1.911). Now line through H parallel to AP: AP direction = P = (1.3765,1.911). So L: (1.215,1.911)+t(1.3765,1.911). We need to check if L is tangent to circle (BEF). Compute distance from O to L. Compute vector O-H = (4.074-1.215, 1.502-1.911) = (2.859, -0.409). Cross product with direction P: (2.859, -0.409) × (1.3765,1.911) = 2.859*1.911 - (-0.409)*1.3765 = 5.460 + 0.563 = 6.023. |P| = sqrt(1.3765^2+1.911^2)=sqrt(1.894+3.652)=sqrt(5.546)=2.355. Given the algebraic approach seems manageable with the formulas we derived, we can attempt to prove the tangency using analytic geometry with the coordinate system we set: A=0, B=2c, M=(c,m), N=(c,n), with c>0, and r^2=c^2+m^2, R^2=c^2+n^2, and m<n, and |m|<|n|? Actually from r<R, we have m^2 < n^2. So |m|<|n|. Since m<n, we have either m negative, n positive, or both positive with m<n. But we can keep general.

We have:

y_C = m - r
y_D = n + R
x_P = (c^2 - y_C y_D)/(2c)
y_P = (y_C + y_D)/2

We also have:

t_E = 2(x_P c + y_P m)/S, where S = x_P^2 + y_P^2
t_F = 2(x_P c + y_P n)/S

Then E = t_E P, F = t_F P.

We also have H = (c + (m-y_P)(n-y_P)/(c-x_P), y_P).

We need to show that the line L: H + t P is tangent to circle through B=(2c,0), E, F.

Now we can compute the circumcircle of BEF. Since E and F are on line through origin with direction P, we can use the earlier derived formulas for the circle equation: x^2+y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0, with D = -2 - 2 (P·M)(P·N)/S? Wait we derived D = -2 - 2 (P·M)(P·N)/S? That was with c=1? Let's re-derive for general c.

We have B=(2c,0). E = t_E P, F = t_F P. Let’s denote P = (p_x, p_y). Then we have:

For point B: (2c)^2 + 0^2 + D(2c) + E*0 + F = 0 => 4c^2 + 2cD + F = 0 => 2cD + F = -4c^2. (1)

For point E: t_E^2 S + D t_E p_x + E t_E p_y + F = 0. (2)
For point F: t_F^2 S + D t_F p_x + E t_F p_y + F = 0. (3)

Subtract (2) and (3): (t_F^2 - t_E^2)S + (t_F - t_E)(D p_x + E p_y) = 0 => (t_F - t_E)[(t_F+t_E)S + (D p_x+E p_y)] = 0. Since t_F ≠ t_E, we have (t_F+t_E)S + (D p_x+E p_y) = 0 => D p_x + E p_y = -(t_F+t_E)S. (4)

Now from (2): t_E^2 S + t_E (D p_x+E p_y) + F = 0 => t_E^2 S + t_E (-(t_F+t_E)S) + F = 0 => t_E^2 S - t_E t_F S - t_E^2 S + F = 0 => - t_E t_F S + F = 0 => F = t_E t_F S. (5)

Now from (1): 2cD + t_E t_F S = -4c^2 => D = (-4c^2 - t_E t_F S)/(2c). (6)

Now we also have (4): D p_x + E p_y = -(t_F+t_E)S. So we can solve for E: E p_y = -(t_F+t_E)S - D p_x => E = [ -(t_F+t_E)S - D p_x ] / p_y, provided p_y ≠ 0.

Now the center O = (-D/2, -E/2). So O_x = -D/2 = (4c^2 + t_E t_F S)/(4c). O_y = -E/2 = [ (t_F+t_E)S + D p_x ]/(2 p_y). (7)

Now we need to show that the distance from O to line L (through H parallel to P) equals radius R_c = sqrt( (D^2+E^2)/4 - F ). Alternatively, we can use the condition that the line L is tangent if and only if the equation for intersection (substituting L into circle equation) has a double root. That is, the quadratic in t from plugging (x_H + t p_x, y_P + t p_y) into circle equation has discriminant zero. That condition is:

( x_H p_x + y_P p_y )^2 - S ( x_H^2 + y_P^2 + D x_H + E y_P + F ) = 0. (8)

Since we have expressions for D, E, F in terms of t_E, t_F, S, p_x, p_y, and also x_H, y_P in terms of m,n,c, we can attempt to verify (8). This is heavy but maybe we can find simplifications by expressing everything in terms of m,n,c and using the relations r^2=c^2+m^2, R^2=c^2+n^2.

We can compute y_C, y_D in terms of m,n,c. Then compute p_x, p_y, S, t_E, t_F. Then compute x_H. Then verify (8). This is a purely algebraic problem. Since we are to produce a rigorous proof, we can do this algebra by hand, but it requires careful manipulation. However we can try to simplify by using the fact that t_E = 2 (p_x c + p_y m)/S, t_F = 2 (p_x c + p_y n)/S. So t_E t_F = 4 (p_x c + p_y m)(p_x c + p_y n)/S^2. And t_E + t_F = 2 (p_x c + p_y m + p_x c + p_y n)/S = 2 (2p_x c + p_y (m+n))/S = (4p_x c + 2p_y (m+n))/S.

Also note that p_x and p_y are given by:

p_x = (c^2 - y_C y_D)/(2c)
p_y = (y_C + y_D)/2

And y_C = m - r, y_D = n + R, with r = sqrt(c^2+m^2), R = sqrt(c^2+n^2). So these are messy.

Maybe we can find a better coordinate system: Place A at origin, and let the line AB be the x-axis. Then B is at (2c,0) as before. But we also have that M and N are on the perpendicular bisector of AB, which is x=c. So M=(c,m), N=(c,n). That's fine.
